Taiwan

Historical Period: 1940-1950

Researcher: Wang Bo-Hsien

The social context includes three main aspects:

  1. Government regulations on religion and legal cases

    1. Social hostility and actions against Christianity

      1. Geopolitical environment and diplomatic relations

Âè∞ÁÅ£Âü∫Áù£Èï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉÂçó„ÄÅÂåóÂ§ßÊúÉÁÇ∫Ë¶Å‰øùÈöúÂÖ∂ÊïôÊúÉÂú∞‰ΩçÔºå1942Âπ¥ÂòóË©¶‰ª•„ÄåÂè∞ÁÅ£Â§ßÊúÉ„ÄçÂêçÁæ©ÂâçÂæÄÊó•Êú¨‰∫§Ê∂âÂä†ÂÖ•Êó•Êú¨Âü∫Áù£ÊïôÂúòÊú™Êûú„ÄÇ1943Âπ¥2Êúà26Êó•ÔºåÂçóÂåóÊïôÊúÉÂÖàË°åËÅØÂêàÔºåÊé°ÊïôÂçÄÂà∂ÂàÜÁÇ∫Âè∞Âåó„ÄÅ‰∏≠ÈÉ®„ÄÅÂè∞Âçó„ÄÅÈ´òÈõÑ4ÊïôÂçÄÔºõÁÑ∂ÂæåÊñº1944Âπ¥4Êúà29Êó•ÔºåÁî±Êó•Êú¨Âü∫Áù£ÊïôÂúòÂè∞ÁÅ£ÊïôÂçÄ„ÄÅÊó•Êú¨ËÅñÂÖ¨ÊúÉÂè∞ÁÅ£ÂÇ≥ÈÅìÂçÄÂèäÂè∞ÁÅ£Âü∫Áù£Èï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉÁ∏ΩÊúÉ‰∏âÂñÆ‰ΩçÂêà‰ΩµËÄåÊàê„ÄåÊó•Êú¨Âü∫Áù£ÊïôÂè∞ÁÅ£ÊïôÂúò„ÄçÔºåÂè∞ÁÅ£Âü∫Áù£ÊïôÂÆåÂÖ®Ê≠∏ÊñºÂêå‰∏ÄÊåáÊèÆÁ≥ªÁµ±ÔºåÂéüÂ±¨Èï∑ËÄÅÊúÉÁ≥ªÁµ±ÁöÑÂçóÂåóÊïôÊúÉÈáçÂøÉÁßªËΩâËá≥Âè∞Âåó„ÄÇ1944Âπ¥7ÊúàÂæåÔºåË®±Â§öÁâßÂ∏´‰πüË¢´ÂæÅÂè¨ÁÇ∫„ÄåË≠∑ÂúãÊå∫Ë∫´ÈöäÂì°„Äç1944Âπ¥11ÊúàÔºåÂè∞ÂåóËøëÈÉäÂÇ≥ÈÅìÂ∏´ÁµÑÊàê„ÄåÊà∞ÊôÇÊÖ∞ÂïèÂêàÂî±Âúò„ÄçÔºå 1945Âπ¥2Êúà13Êó•ÔºåÂè∞ÁÅ£ÊïôÂúòÁµÑ„ÄåÊà∞ÊôÇÊå∫Ë∫´Èöä„ÄçÔºåË¶ÅÊ±ÇÈöäÂì°Êèê‰æõÊÉÖÂ†±Ëá≥Á∏ΩÈÉ®Ôºå1945Âπ¥8Êúà15Êó•Êà∞Áà≠ÁµêÊùüÔºå„ÄåÊó•Êú¨Âü∫Áù£ÊïôÂè∞ÁÅ£ÊïôÂúò„ÄçËß£Êï£ÔºåÈÅéÂéªÂÇ≥ÊïôËÄÖÂøÖÈ†àÁÇ∫ÊîøÂ∫úÊïàÂäõÁöÑÈõôÈáçË∫´ÂàÜ‰πüÊâçÂæó‰ª•Ëß£Èô§„ÄÇÔºàÂæêË¨ô‰ø°Ôºå1965Ôºå265-266Ôºâ

Wartime Policies on Religion in Taiwan (1940-1950)

During wartime, the Japanese government not only granted State Shinto a privileged status but also tightened control over other religions. The most comprehensive regulation was the Religious Juridical Persons Act (ÂÆóÊïôÊ≥ï‰∫∫Ê≥ï, Êò≠Âíå26Âπ¥Ê≥ïÂæãÁ¨¨126Âè∑), enacted in 1939, which encompassed all ‚ÄúShinto sects, Buddhist sects, religious organizations, churches, religious groups, dioceses, and other similar organizations‚Äù (Article 2-1). After the Public Security Preservation Law (Ê≤ªÂÆâÁ∂≠ÊåÅÊ≥ï) was revised in 1941, penalties were intensified, and the scope of suppression was expanded, further increasing religious persecution.

By that time, Japan had entered a full-scale wartime mobilization system, especially following the enactment of the National Mobilization Law (ÂõΩÂÆ∂Á∑èÂãïÂì°Ê≥ï) in 1938, which integrated Japan‚Äôs national economy into wartime conditions. The legal framework also promoted National Spiritual Mobilization (ÂõΩÊ∞ëÁ≤æÁ•ûÁ∑èÂãïÂì°), making it difficult for religious activities and doctrines to remain unaffected. The religious control policies were guided by the following principles:

  1. Upholding the national polity and eliminating irreverent ideologies.

    1. Preventing public unrest caused by religious beliefs.

      1. Maintaining fundamental economic structures.

        1. Prohibiting morally disruptive behaviors.

          (Sasaki Toshiji, 1966, p.49; Nagai Kiyoko, 1965, p.237)

          Suppression of Religious Practices in Taiwan

          In 1936, Governor-General of Taiwan, Kobayashi Seiz≈ç, held the Grand Shrine Amulet Distribution Ceremony at the official residence, emphasizing the importance of household worship and faith. Following this, local authorities initiated campaigns to suppress traditional Taiwanese beliefs, including the Official Hall Reform Movement and the Temple Reorganization Movement. The Official Hall Reform Movement primarily required every household to install a kamidana (Shinto household altar) and enshrine the Grand Shrine Amulet (jing≈´ taima). Meanwhile, the handling of ancestral tablets and religious statues varied by locality.

          (Tsai Chin-Tang, 1991, pp. 65-72)

          For Christians, the conflict between their faith and state ideology became increasingly apparent. The Northern Presbyterian Church‚Äôs declaration on the national situation was seen as overly sycophantic. On the issue of Emperor worship, the Northern Church frequently justified participation by claiming that ‚Äúnational rituals are non-religious or transcend religious patriotism.‚Äù They also took the initiative to sever ties with their Canadian mother church.

          Another major challenge was the Shinto shrine worship requirement for Christian schools. Throughout the 1930s, churches in the north and south adopted different approaches to address this challenge. By 1940, due to its religious stance, Tainan Theological Seminary refused to compromise with the authorities, leading to its closure and merger with Taipei Theological Seminary. All Western missionaries were forced to return to their home countries, and Taiwanese churches entered a phase of complete self-governance, self-propagation, and self-financing.

          In November 1940, the Japan Christian Alliance held its 18th General Assembly, officially admitting the Taiwanese church. However, under the influence of State Shinto, the government organized three Christian Missionary Training Conferences across Taiwan starting that year. Presbyterian pastors and preachers were compelled to attend these sessions, where they were indoctrinated with Shinto principles and nationalistic ideologies. Those forced to participate, such as Pastors Huang Wu-Tung and Hsu Chien-Hsin, later documented their suffering in written accounts.

          (Hsu Chien-Hsin, 1965, pp. 262-264)

          The Pacific War and Christian Repression

          In 1941, with the outbreak of the Pacific War, the Government-General of Taiwan promoted the concepts of Loyalty and Sacrifice and Selfless Devotion, establishing the Imperial Subjects Service Association (ÁöáÊ∞ëÂ•âÂÖ¨‰ºö), which integrated the state, Shinto, the Emperor, and the Japanese spirit, further suppressing traditional religious practices while expanding societal mobilization in Taiwan.

          In 1942, this organization expanded into the Taiwan Christian Service Association, later renamed the Taiwan Christian Service Corps, with the ultimate goal of merging all Christian groups into a single Taiwan Christian Church.

          (Hsu Chien-Hsin, 1965, pp. 262-264)

          In an effort to secure their position, the Northern and Southern Synods of the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan attempted in 1942 to join the United Church of Japan under the name Taiwan Assembly, but their request was rejected. On February 26, 1943, the northern and southern churches united under a diocesan system, dividing Taiwan into four districts: Taipei, Central, Tainan, and Kaohsiung.

          Then, on April 29, 1944, the United Church of Japan Taiwan Diocese, the Taiwan District of the Nippon Sei Ko Kai (Anglican Church), and the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan were forcibly merged to form the Japan Christian Church Taiwan Diocese, bringing all Taiwanese Christian institutions under a single command structure. The leadership of the Presbyterian Church shifted to Taipei.

          After July 1944, many pastors were conscripted into the National Defense Mobilization Corps. In November 1944, preachers near Taipei formed a Wartime Consolation Choir. On February 13, 1945, the Taiwan Christian Service Corps established a Wartime Mobilization Unit, requiring members to provide intelligence reports to headquarters.

          With Japan‚Äôs surrender on August 15, 1945, the Japan Christian Church Taiwan Diocese was dissolved, and the obligation of Christian missionaries to serve the government was finally lifted.

          (Hsu Chien-Hsin, 1965, pp. 265-266)

          1. Ë´ãÊèèËø∞ÈÄôÂçÅÂπ¥Èñì‰∏≠ÂúãÁ§æÊúÉÈóúÊñºÊïµË¶ñÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÁöÑÁöÑÁõ∏ÈóúÊÉÖÊ≥ÅÔºåÂåÖÊã¨ËàáÁâπÂÆöÂúòÈ´îÁöÑË°ùÁ™ÅÂíåËûçÂêàÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÁöÑÂä™ÂäõÊÉÖÊ≥ÅÁ≠â ÔºàÊØèÂÄã‰∫ã‰ª∂500-1,000Â≠óÔºâ„ÄÇ ‚òí

            1. Ë´ãÂú®ÊñáÊ™îÊú´„ÄåÊñáÁçª„Äç ËôïÊ∑ªÂä†Áõ∏ÈóúÊñáÁçªÁöÑÂá∫ËôïÔºàËá≥Â∞ë‰∏ÄËôïÂá∫ËôïÔºåË∂äÂ§öË∂äÂ•Ω„ÄÇ Â¶ÇÂèØËÉΩÔºå‰πüË´ãÂåÖÊã¨‰∏≠Êó•ÈüìËã±ÊàñÂÖ∂‰ªñÊ≠êÊ¥≤Ë™ûË®ÄÁöÑÊñáÁçªÂá∫ËôïÔºâ„ÄÇ ‚òí

          ÊÇ®ÁöÑÂõûÁ≠îÔºö

          Êú¨ÊôÇÊúüÁÇ∫Êó•Êú¨ÊôÇ‰ª£Âà∞ÂúãÊ∞ëÊîøÂ∫ú‰æÜÂè∞Èñì‰πãÈÅéÊ∏°ÊôÇÊúüÔºåÊó•Êú¨Âú®‰∫åÊà∞ÊúÄÂæåÁöÑÁöáÊ∞ëÂåñÊîøÁ≠ñÊñºÂâç‰∏ÄÊôÇÊúüÂ∑≤Ë®éË´ñÔºåËÄåÂúãÊ∞ëÈª®ÊîøÂ∫úÂú®Âè∞Â∞çÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÁöÑÂÑ™ÂæÖ‰πüÂ∞öÊú™ÊòéÈ°ØÂá∫ÁèæÔºåÊîøÊ≤ªÁ¥õÊìæÁöÑÂ§ßÁí∞Â¢É‰∏ãÂú®Âè∞ÁÅ£ÁÑ°ÊòéÈ°ØÂ∞çÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÁöÑÁ§æÊúÉÊïµË¶ñÊÉÖÁ∑íÂíåË°åÂãï„ÄÇ

          This period marks the transition from Japanese rule to the arrival of the Nationalist government in Taiwan. The final K≈çminka (imperialization) policies implemented by Japan during World War II were discussed in the previous section, while the preferential treatment of Christianity by the Kuomintang (KMT) government in Taiwan had yet to become evident. Amidst the political turbulence, there were no significant manifestations of social hostility or actions against Christianity in Taiwan during this transitional phase.

          3.Âú∞Á∑£ÊîøÊ≤ªÁí∞Â¢ÉÂíåÂ§ñ‰∫§Èóú‰øÇ

          Ë´ãÂú®‰∏ãÊñπ „ÄåÊÇ®ÁöÑÂõûÁ≠î„Äç ÈÉ®ÂàÜÊèèËø∞Âú®ÈÄôÂçÅÂπ¥ÈñìÔºåÊúÄÊúâÂΩ±ÈüøÂäõÁöÑÂú∞Á∑£ÊîøÊ≤ªÂãïÊÖãÂíåÂ§ñ‰∫§Èóú‰øÇ„ÄÇ

          ÔºàË´ãÂú®ÂÆåÊàêÁõ∏ÊáâÁöÑË¶ÅÊ±ÇÂêéÊ®ôË®òÊñáÂêéË§áÈÅ∏Ê°ÜÔºâÔºö

          The Nationalist Government‚Äôs Military Takeover of Taiwan

          On August 15, 1945, Japan announced its acceptance of the Potsdam Declaration and surrendered. Following the war, the Nationalist government, under the General Order No. One issued by the Allied forces, was granted the authority to accept Japan‚Äôs surrender in Taiwan, which had not previously been part of China‚Äôs military zone. On October 25, 1945, the Republic of China‚Äôs Nationalist government officially took over Taiwan and the Penghu Islands, marking the end of the Japanese colonial period and the beginning of the postwar era.

          However, shortly after Taiwan‚Äôs so-called recovery (guangfu), the government became plagued by widespread corruption, military and political personnel exhibited poor discipline, and the administration imposed a controlled economy that restricted private economic activity. The Nationalist government transferred resources seized in Taiwan to mainland China to support its ongoing civil war against the Chinese Communist Party. This resulted in a more than tenfold increase in prices, exacerbated by the excessive printing of Taiwanese currency to cover government expenses, leading to severe inflation and profoundly impacting Taiwan‚Äôs economy, fueling public resentment.

          (Wu Tsung-Min, 2006, pp. 129-159)

          Following the principle of postwar reparations, 237 state-owned and private Japanese enterprises, along with over 600 industrial units, were placed under the management of 27 companies established within various bureaus of the Taiwan Provincial Administrative Office. However, due to large-scale mismanagement and corruption, these Japanese assets were transformed into private property for the privileged elite or were auctioned off. Taiwanese private property and businesses were also unjustly confiscated.

          (Lai Tze-Han, 1994, pp. 3-27)

          The Nationalist government was widely criticized for ruling Taiwan with the attitude of a conqueror, governing with a sense of superiority and arbitrary authority. The Taiwanese population suffered from discrimination and unfair treatment by the newly arrived mainland Chinese military and political officials, leading to widespread dissatisfaction with the Nationalist government. This mounting resentment eventually erupted into large-scale protests.

          (Teng Kung-Chao, 1991, p. 29)

          Between February 27 and May 16, 1947, violent clashes between government officials and civilians broke out across Taiwan as citizens demanded political reforms. In response, the Nationalist government deployed military forces to suppress the unrest, in what became known as the February 28 Incident (Er-er-ba Shijian). Many intellectuals who had initially welcomed Nationalist rule and resisted Japanese colonialism were either killed, persecuted, or forced into exile, dying away from their homeland.

Taiwan

Historical Period: 1940-1950

Researcher: Wang Bo-Hsien

The social context includes three main aspects:

  1. Government regulations on religion and legal cases

    1. Social hostility and actions against Christianity

      1. Geopolitical environment and diplomatic relations

Âè∞ÁÅ£Âü∫Áù£Èï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉÂçó„ÄÅÂåóÂ§ßÊúÉÁÇ∫Ë¶Å‰øùÈöúÂÖ∂ÊïôÊúÉÂú∞‰ΩçÔºå1942Âπ¥ÂòóË©¶‰ª•„ÄåÂè∞ÁÅ£Â§ßÊúÉ„ÄçÂêçÁæ©ÂâçÂæÄÊó•Êú¨‰∫§Ê∂âÂä†ÂÖ•Êó•Êú¨Âü∫Áù£ÊïôÂúòÊú™Êûú„ÄÇ1943Âπ¥2Êúà26Êó•ÔºåÂçóÂåóÊïôÊúÉÂÖàË°åËÅØÂêàÔºåÊé°ÊïôÂçÄÂà∂ÂàÜÁÇ∫Âè∞Âåó„ÄÅ‰∏≠ÈÉ®„ÄÅÂè∞Âçó„ÄÅÈ´òÈõÑ4ÊïôÂçÄÔºõÁÑ∂ÂæåÊñº1944Âπ¥4Êúà29Êó•ÔºåÁî±Êó•Êú¨Âü∫Áù£ÊïôÂúòÂè∞ÁÅ£ÊïôÂçÄ„ÄÅÊó•Êú¨ËÅñÂÖ¨ÊúÉÂè∞ÁÅ£ÂÇ≥ÈÅìÂçÄÂèäÂè∞ÁÅ£Âü∫Áù£Èï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉÁ∏ΩÊúÉ‰∏âÂñÆ‰ΩçÂêà‰ΩµËÄåÊàê„ÄåÊó•Êú¨Âü∫Áù£ÊïôÂè∞ÁÅ£ÊïôÂúò„ÄçÔºåÂè∞ÁÅ£Âü∫Áù£ÊïôÂÆåÂÖ®Ê≠∏ÊñºÂêå‰∏ÄÊåáÊèÆÁ≥ªÁµ±ÔºåÂéüÂ±¨Èï∑ËÄÅÊúÉÁ≥ªÁµ±ÁöÑÂçóÂåóÊïôÊúÉÈáçÂøÉÁßªËΩâËá≥Âè∞Âåó„ÄÇ1944Âπ¥7ÊúàÂæåÔºåË®±Â§öÁâßÂ∏´‰πüË¢´ÂæÅÂè¨ÁÇ∫„ÄåË≠∑ÂúãÊå∫Ë∫´ÈöäÂì°„Äç1944Âπ¥11ÊúàÔºåÂè∞ÂåóËøëÈÉäÂÇ≥ÈÅìÂ∏´ÁµÑÊàê„ÄåÊà∞ÊôÇÊÖ∞ÂïèÂêàÂî±Âúò„ÄçÔºå 1945Âπ¥2Êúà13Êó•ÔºåÂè∞ÁÅ£ÊïôÂúòÁµÑ„ÄåÊà∞ÊôÇÊå∫Ë∫´Èöä„ÄçÔºåË¶ÅÊ±ÇÈöäÂì°Êèê‰æõÊÉÖÂ†±Ëá≥Á∏ΩÈÉ®Ôºå1945Âπ¥8Êúà15Êó•Êà∞Áà≠ÁµêÊùüÔºå„ÄåÊó•Êú¨Âü∫Áù£ÊïôÂè∞ÁÅ£ÊïôÂúò„ÄçËß£Êï£ÔºåÈÅéÂéªÂÇ≥ÊïôËÄÖÂøÖÈ†àÁÇ∫ÊîøÂ∫úÊïàÂäõÁöÑÈõôÈáçË∫´ÂàÜ‰πüÊâçÂæó‰ª•Ëß£Èô§„ÄÇÔºàÂæêË¨ô‰ø°Ôºå1965Ôºå265-266Ôºâ

Wartime Policies on Religion in Taiwan (1940-1950)

During wartime, the Japanese government not only granted State Shinto a privileged status but also tightened control over other religions. The most comprehensive regulation was the Religious Juridical Persons Act (ÂÆóÊïôÊ≥ï‰∫∫Ê≥ï, Êò≠Âíå26Âπ¥Ê≥ïÂæãÁ¨¨126Âè∑), enacted in 1939, which encompassed all ‚ÄúShinto sects, Buddhist sects, religious organizations, churches, religious groups, dioceses, and other similar organizations‚Äù (Article 2-1). After the Public Security Preservation Law (Ê≤ªÂÆâÁ∂≠ÊåÅÊ≥ï) was revised in 1941, penalties were intensified, and the scope of suppression was expanded, further increasing religious persecution.

By that time, Japan had entered a full-scale wartime mobilization system, especially following the enactment of the National Mobilization Law (ÂõΩÂÆ∂Á∑èÂãïÂì°Ê≥ï) in 1938, which integrated Japan‚Äôs national economy into wartime conditions. The legal framework also promoted National Spiritual Mobilization (ÂõΩÊ∞ëÁ≤æÁ•ûÁ∑èÂãïÂì°), making it difficult for religious activities and doctrines to remain unaffected. The religious control policies were guided by the following principles:

  1. Upholding the national polity and eliminating irreverent ideologies.

    1. Preventing public unrest caused by religious beliefs.

      1. Maintaining fundamental economic structures.

        1. Prohibiting morally disruptive behaviors.

          (Sasaki Toshiji, 1966, p.49; Nagai Kiyoko, 1965, p.237)

          Suppression of Religious Practices in Taiwan

          In 1936, Governor-General of Taiwan, Kobayashi Seiz≈ç, held the Grand Shrine Amulet Distribution Ceremony at the official residence, emphasizing the importance of household worship and faith. Following this, local authorities initiated campaigns to suppress traditional Taiwanese beliefs, including the Official Hall Reform Movement and the Temple Reorganization Movement. The Official Hall Reform Movement primarily required every household to install a kamidana (Shinto household altar) and enshrine the Grand Shrine Amulet (jing≈´ taima). Meanwhile, the handling of ancestral tablets and religious statues varied by locality.

          (Tsai Chin-Tang, 1991, pp. 65-72)

          For Christians, the conflict between their faith and state ideology became increasingly apparent. The Northern Presbyterian Church‚Äôs declaration on the national situation was seen as overly sycophantic. On the issue of Emperor worship, the Northern Church frequently justified participation by claiming that ‚Äúnational rituals are non-religious or transcend religious patriotism.‚Äù They also took the initiative to sever ties with their Canadian mother church.

          Another major challenge was the Shinto shrine worship requirement for Christian schools. Throughout the 1930s, churches in the north and south adopted different approaches to address this challenge. By 1940, due to its religious stance, Tainan Theological Seminary refused to compromise with the authorities, leading to its closure and merger with Taipei Theological Seminary. All Western missionaries were forced to return to their home countries, and Taiwanese churches entered a phase of complete self-governance, self-propagation, and self-financing.

          In November 1940, the Japan Christian Alliance held its 18th General Assembly, officially admitting the Taiwanese church. However, under the influence of State Shinto, the government organized three Christian Missionary Training Conferences across Taiwan starting that year. Presbyterian pastors and preachers were compelled to attend these sessions, where they were indoctrinated with Shinto principles and nationalistic ideologies. Those forced to participate, such as Pastors Huang Wu-Tung and Hsu Chien-Hsin, later documented their suffering in written accounts.

          (Hsu Chien-Hsin, 1965, pp. 262-264)

          The Pacific War and Christian Repression

          In 1941, with the outbreak of the Pacific War, the Government-General of Taiwan promoted the concepts of Loyalty and Sacrifice and Selfless Devotion, establishing the Imperial Subjects Service Association (ÁöáÊ∞ëÂ•âÂÖ¨‰ºö), which integrated the state, Shinto, the Emperor, and the Japanese spirit, further suppressing traditional religious practices while expanding societal mobilization in Taiwan.

          In 1942, this organization expanded into the Taiwan Christian Service Association, later renamed the Taiwan Christian Service Corps, with the ultimate goal of merging all Christian groups into a single Taiwan Christian Church.

          (Hsu Chien-Hsin, 1965, pp. 262-264)

          In an effort to secure their position, the Northern and Southern Synods of the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan attempted in 1942 to join the United Church of Japan under the name Taiwan Assembly, but their request was rejected. On February 26, 1943, the northern and southern churches united under a diocesan system, dividing Taiwan into four districts: Taipei, Central, Tainan, and Kaohsiung.

          Then, on April 29, 1944, the United Church of Japan Taiwan Diocese, the Taiwan District of the Nippon Sei Ko Kai (Anglican Church), and the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan were forcibly merged to form the Japan Christian Church Taiwan Diocese, bringing all Taiwanese Christian institutions under a single command structure. The leadership of the Presbyterian Church shifted to Taipei.

          After July 1944, many pastors were conscripted into the National Defense Mobilization Corps. In November 1944, preachers near Taipei formed a Wartime Consolation Choir. On February 13, 1945, the Taiwan Christian Service Corps established a Wartime Mobilization Unit, requiring members to provide intelligence reports to headquarters.

          With Japan‚Äôs surrender on August 15, 1945, the Japan Christian Church Taiwan Diocese was dissolved, and the obligation of Christian missionaries to serve the government was finally lifted.

          (Hsu Chien-Hsin, 1965, pp. 265-266)

            1. Ë´ãÂú®ÊñáÊ™îÊú´„ÄåÊñáÁçª„Äç ËôïÊ∑ªÂä†Áõ∏ÈóúÊñáÁçªÁöÑÂá∫ËôïÔºàËá≥Â∞ë‰∏ÄËôïÂá∫ËôïÔºåË∂äÂ§öË∂äÂ•Ω„ÄÇ Â¶ÇÂèØËÉΩÔºå‰πüË´ãÂåÖÊã¨‰∏≠Êó•ÈüìËã±ÊàñÂÖ∂‰ªñÊ≠êÊ¥≤Ë™ûË®ÄÁöÑÊñáÁçªÂá∫ËôïÔºâ„ÄÇ ‚òí

          ÊÇ®ÁöÑÂõûÁ≠îÔºö

          Êú¨ÊôÇÊúüÁÇ∫Êó•Êú¨ÊôÇ‰ª£Âà∞ÂúãÊ∞ëÊîøÂ∫ú‰æÜÂè∞Èñì‰πãÈÅéÊ∏°ÊôÇÊúüÔºåÊó•Êú¨Âú®‰∫åÊà∞ÊúÄÂæåÁöÑÁöáÊ∞ëÂåñÊîøÁ≠ñÊñºÂâç‰∏ÄÊôÇÊúüÂ∑≤Ë®éË´ñÔºåËÄåÂúãÊ∞ëÈª®ÊîøÂ∫úÂú®Âè∞Â∞çÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÁöÑÂÑ™ÂæÖ‰πüÂ∞öÊú™ÊòéÈ°ØÂá∫ÁèæÔºåÊîøÊ≤ªÁ¥õÊìæÁöÑÂ§ßÁí∞Â¢É‰∏ãÂú®Âè∞ÁÅ£ÁÑ°ÊòéÈ°ØÂ∞çÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÁöÑÁ§æÊúÉÊïµË¶ñÊÉÖÁ∑íÂíåË°åÂãï„ÄÇ

          This period marks the transition from Japanese rule to the arrival of the Nationalist government in Taiwan. The final K≈çminka (imperialization) policies implemented by Japan during World War II were discussed in the previous section, while the preferential treatment of Christianity by the Kuomintang (KMT) government in Taiwan had yet to become evident. Amidst the political turbulence, there were no significant manifestations of social hostility or actions against Christianity in Taiwan during this transitional phase.

          The Nationalist Government‚Äôs Military Takeover of Taiwan

          On August 15, 1945, Japan announced its acceptance of the Potsdam Declaration and surrendered. Following the war, the Nationalist government, under the General Order No. One issued by the Allied forces, was granted the authority to accept Japan‚Äôs surrender in Taiwan, which had not previously been part of China‚Äôs military zone. On October 25, 1945, the Republic of China‚Äôs Nationalist government officially took over Taiwan and the Penghu Islands, marking the end of the Japanese colonial period and the beginning of the postwar era.

          However, shortly after Taiwan‚Äôs so-called recovery (guangfu), the government became plagued by widespread corruption, military and political personnel exhibited poor discipline, and the administration imposed a controlled economy that restricted private economic activity. The Nationalist government transferred resources seized in Taiwan to mainland China to support its ongoing civil war against the Chinese Communist Party. This resulted in a more than tenfold increase in prices, exacerbated by the excessive printing of Taiwanese currency to cover government expenses, leading to severe inflation and profoundly impacting Taiwan‚Äôs economy, fueling public resentment.

          (Wu Tsung-Min, 2006, pp. 129-159)

          Following the principle of postwar reparations, 237 state-owned and private Japanese enterprises, along with over 600 industrial units, were placed under the management of 27 companies established within various bureaus of the Taiwan Provincial Administrative Office. However, due to large-scale mismanagement and corruption, these Japanese assets were transformed into private property for the privileged elite or were auctioned off. Taiwanese private property and businesses were also unjustly confiscated.

          (Lai Tze-Han, 1994, pp. 3-27)

          The Nationalist government was widely criticized for ruling Taiwan with the attitude of a conqueror, governing with a sense of superiority and arbitrary authority. The Taiwanese population suffered from discrimination and unfair treatment by the newly arrived mainland Chinese military and political officials, leading to widespread dissatisfaction with the Nationalist government. This mounting resentment eventually erupted into large-scale protests.

          (Teng Kung-Chao, 1991, p. 29)

          Between February 27 and May 16, 1947, violent clashes between government officials and civilians broke out across Taiwan as citizens demanded political reforms. In response, the Nationalist government deployed military forces to suppress the unrest, in what became known as the February 28 Incident (Er-er-ba Shijian). Many intellectuals who had initially welcomed Nationalist rule and resisted Japanese colonialism were either killed, persecuted, or forced into exile, dying away from their homeland.

Geopolitical Dynamics and International Relationships

Taiwan

Historical Period: 1940-1950

Researcher: Wang Bo-Hsien

The social context includes three main aspects:

  1. Government regulations on religion and legal cases

    1. Social hostility and actions against Christianity

Âè∞ÁÅ£Âü∫Áù£Èï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉÂçó„ÄÅÂåóÂ§ßÊúÉÁÇ∫Ë¶Å‰øùÈöúÂÖ∂ÊïôÊúÉÂú∞‰ΩçÔºå1942Âπ¥ÂòóË©¶‰ª•„ÄåÂè∞ÁÅ£Â§ßÊúÉ„ÄçÂêçÁæ©ÂâçÂæÄÊó•Êú¨‰∫§Ê∂âÂä†ÂÖ•Êó•Êú¨Âü∫Áù£ÊïôÂúòÊú™Êûú„ÄÇ1943Âπ¥2Êúà26Êó•ÔºåÂçóÂåóÊïôÊúÉÂÖàË°åËÅØÂêàÔºåÊé°ÊïôÂçÄÂà∂ÂàÜÁÇ∫Âè∞Âåó„ÄÅ‰∏≠ÈÉ®„ÄÅÂè∞Âçó„ÄÅÈ´òÈõÑ4ÊïôÂçÄÔºõÁÑ∂ÂæåÊñº1944Âπ¥4Êúà29Êó•ÔºåÁî±Êó•Êú¨Âü∫Áù£ÊïôÂúòÂè∞ÁÅ£ÊïôÂçÄ„ÄÅÊó•Êú¨ËÅñÂÖ¨ÊúÉÂè∞ÁÅ£ÂÇ≥ÈÅìÂçÄÂèäÂè∞ÁÅ£Âü∫Áù£Èï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉÁ∏ΩÊúÉ‰∏âÂñÆ‰ΩçÂêà‰ΩµËÄåÊàê„ÄåÊó•Êú¨Âü∫Áù£ÊïôÂè∞ÁÅ£ÊïôÂúò„ÄçÔºåÂè∞ÁÅ£Âü∫Áù£ÊïôÂÆåÂÖ®Ê≠∏ÊñºÂêå‰∏ÄÊåáÊèÆÁ≥ªÁµ±ÔºåÂéüÂ±¨Èï∑ËÄÅÊúÉÁ≥ªÁµ±ÁöÑÂçóÂåóÊïôÊúÉÈáçÂøÉÁßªËΩâËá≥Âè∞Âåó„ÄÇ1944Âπ¥7ÊúàÂæåÔºåË®±Â§öÁâßÂ∏´‰πüË¢´ÂæÅÂè¨ÁÇ∫„ÄåË≠∑ÂúãÊå∫Ë∫´ÈöäÂì°„Äç1944Âπ¥11ÊúàÔºåÂè∞ÂåóËøëÈÉäÂÇ≥ÈÅìÂ∏´ÁµÑÊàê„ÄåÊà∞ÊôÇÊÖ∞ÂïèÂêàÂî±Âúò„ÄçÔºå 1945Âπ¥2Êúà13Êó•ÔºåÂè∞ÁÅ£ÊïôÂúòÁµÑ„ÄåÊà∞ÊôÇÊå∫Ë∫´Èöä„ÄçÔºåË¶ÅÊ±ÇÈöäÂì°Êèê‰æõÊÉÖÂ†±Ëá≥Á∏ΩÈÉ®Ôºå1945Âπ¥8Êúà15Êó•Êà∞Áà≠ÁµêÊùüÔºå„ÄåÊó•Êú¨Âü∫Áù£ÊïôÂè∞ÁÅ£ÊïôÂúò„ÄçËß£Êï£ÔºåÈÅéÂéªÂÇ≥ÊïôËÄÖÂøÖÈ†àÁÇ∫ÊîøÂ∫úÊïàÂäõÁöÑÈõôÈáçË∫´ÂàÜ‰πüÊâçÂæó‰ª•Ëß£Èô§„ÄÇÔºàÂæêË¨ô‰ø°Ôºå1965Ôºå265-266Ôºâ

Wartime Policies on Religion in Taiwan (1940-1950)

During wartime, the Japanese government not only granted State Shinto a privileged status but also tightened control over other religions. The most comprehensive regulation was the Religious Juridical Persons Act (ÂÆóÊïôÊ≥ï‰∫∫Ê≥ï, Êò≠Âíå26Âπ¥Ê≥ïÂæãÁ¨¨126Âè∑), enacted in 1939, which encompassed all ‚ÄúShinto sects, Buddhist sects, religious organizations, churches, religious groups, dioceses, and other similar organizations‚Äù (Article 2-1). After the Public Security Preservation Law (Ê≤ªÂÆâÁ∂≠ÊåÅÊ≥ï) was revised in 1941, penalties were intensified, and the scope of suppression was expanded, further increasing religious persecution.

By that time, Japan had entered a full-scale wartime mobilization system, especially following the enactment of the National Mobilization Law (ÂõΩÂÆ∂Á∑èÂãïÂì°Ê≥ï) in 1938, which integrated Japan‚Äôs national economy into wartime conditions. The legal framework also promoted National Spiritual Mobilization (ÂõΩÊ∞ëÁ≤æÁ•ûÁ∑èÂãïÂì°), making it difficult for religious activities and doctrines to remain unaffected. The religious control policies were guided by the following principles:

  1. Upholding the national polity and eliminating irreverent ideologies.

    1. Preventing public unrest caused by religious beliefs.

      1. Maintaining fundamental economic structures.

        1. Prohibiting morally disruptive behaviors.

          (Sasaki Toshiji, 1966, p.49; Nagai Kiyoko, 1965, p.237)

          Suppression of Religious Practices in Taiwan

          In 1936, Governor-General of Taiwan, Kobayashi Seiz≈ç, held the Grand Shrine Amulet Distribution Ceremony at the official residence, emphasizing the importance of household worship and faith. Following this, local authorities initiated campaigns to suppress traditional Taiwanese beliefs, including the Official Hall Reform Movement and the Temple Reorganization Movement. The Official Hall Reform Movement primarily required every household to install a kamidana (Shinto household altar) and enshrine the Grand Shrine Amulet (jing≈´ taima). Meanwhile, the handling of ancestral tablets and religious statues varied by locality.

          (Tsai Chin-Tang, 1991, pp. 65-72)

          For Christians, the conflict between their faith and state ideology became increasingly apparent. The Northern Presbyterian Church‚Äôs declaration on the national situation was seen as overly sycophantic. On the issue of Emperor worship, the Northern Church frequently justified participation by claiming that ‚Äúnational rituals are non-religious or transcend religious patriotism.‚Äù They also took the initiative to sever ties with their Canadian mother church.

          Another major challenge was the Shinto shrine worship requirement for Christian schools. Throughout the 1930s, churches in the north and south adopted different approaches to address this challenge. By 1940, due to its religious stance, Tainan Theological Seminary refused to compromise with the authorities, leading to its closure and merger with Taipei Theological Seminary. All Western missionaries were forced to return to their home countries, and Taiwanese churches entered a phase of complete self-governance, self-propagation, and self-financing.

          In November 1940, the Japan Christian Alliance held its 18th General Assembly, officially admitting the Taiwanese church. However, under the influence of State Shinto, the government organized three Christian Missionary Training Conferences across Taiwan starting that year. Presbyterian pastors and preachers were compelled to attend these sessions, where they were indoctrinated with Shinto principles and nationalistic ideologies. Those forced to participate, such as Pastors Huang Wu-Tung and Hsu Chien-Hsin, later documented their suffering in written accounts.

          (Hsu Chien-Hsin, 1965, pp. 262-264)

          The Pacific War and Christian Repression

          In 1941, with the outbreak of the Pacific War, the Government-General of Taiwan promoted the concepts of Loyalty and Sacrifice and Selfless Devotion, establishing the Imperial Subjects Service Association (ÁöáÊ∞ëÂ•âÂÖ¨‰ºö), which integrated the state, Shinto, the Emperor, and the Japanese spirit, further suppressing traditional religious practices while expanding societal mobilization in Taiwan.

          In 1942, this organization expanded into the Taiwan Christian Service Association, later renamed the Taiwan Christian Service Corps, with the ultimate goal of merging all Christian groups into a single Taiwan Christian Church.

          (Hsu Chien-Hsin, 1965, pp. 262-264)

          In an effort to secure their position, the Northern and Southern Synods of the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan attempted in 1942 to join the United Church of Japan under the name Taiwan Assembly, but their request was rejected. On February 26, 1943, the northern and southern churches united under a diocesan system, dividing Taiwan into four districts: Taipei, Central, Tainan, and Kaohsiung.

          Then, on April 29, 1944, the United Church of Japan Taiwan Diocese, the Taiwan District of the Nippon Sei Ko Kai (Anglican Church), and the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan were forcibly merged to form the Japan Christian Church Taiwan Diocese, bringing all Taiwanese Christian institutions under a single command structure. The leadership of the Presbyterian Church shifted to Taipei.

          After July 1944, many pastors were conscripted into the National Defense Mobilization Corps. In November 1944, preachers near Taipei formed a Wartime Consolation Choir. On February 13, 1945, the Taiwan Christian Service Corps established a Wartime Mobilization Unit, requiring members to provide intelligence reports to headquarters.

          With Japan‚Äôs surrender on August 15, 1945, the Japan Christian Church Taiwan Diocese was dissolved, and the obligation of Christian missionaries to serve the government was finally lifted.

          (Hsu Chien-Hsin, 1965, pp. 265-266)

          ÊÇ®ÁöÑÂõûÁ≠îÔºö

          Êú¨ÊôÇÊúüÁÇ∫Êó•Êú¨ÊôÇ‰ª£Âà∞ÂúãÊ∞ëÊîøÂ∫ú‰æÜÂè∞Èñì‰πãÈÅéÊ∏°ÊôÇÊúüÔºåÊó•Êú¨Âú®‰∫åÊà∞ÊúÄÂæåÁöÑÁöáÊ∞ëÂåñÊîøÁ≠ñÊñºÂâç‰∏ÄÊôÇÊúüÂ∑≤Ë®éË´ñÔºåËÄåÂúãÊ∞ëÈª®ÊîøÂ∫úÂú®Âè∞Â∞çÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÁöÑÂÑ™ÂæÖ‰πüÂ∞öÊú™ÊòéÈ°ØÂá∫ÁèæÔºåÊîøÊ≤ªÁ¥õÊìæÁöÑÂ§ßÁí∞Â¢É‰∏ãÂú®Âè∞ÁÅ£ÁÑ°ÊòéÈ°ØÂ∞çÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÁöÑÁ§æÊúÉÊïµË¶ñÊÉÖÁ∑íÂíåË°åÂãï„ÄÇ

          This period marks the transition from Japanese rule to the arrival of the Nationalist government in Taiwan. The final K≈çminka (imperialization) policies implemented by Japan during World War II were discussed in the previous section, while the preferential treatment of Christianity by the Kuomintang (KMT) government in Taiwan had yet to become evident. Amidst the political turbulence, there were no significant manifestations of social hostility or actions against Christianity in Taiwan during this transitional phase.

          1945 Âπ¥ 8 Êúà 15 Êó•ÔºåÊó•Êú¨ÂÆ£ÂëäÊé•ÂèóÊ≥¢Ëå®Âù¶ÂÆ£Ë®ÄÊäïÈôçÔºåÁµÇÊà∞ÂæåÂúãÊ∞ëÊîøÂ∫úÊìöÁõüËªçÁöÑ„Äå‰∏ÄËà¨ÂëΩ‰ª§Á¨¨ 1 ËôüÔºàGeneral Order no.           ÁÑ∂ËÄåËá∫ÁÅ£„ÄåÂÖâÂæ©„Äç‰∏ç‰πÖÂæåÔºåÊîøÂ∫úÈ†ªÂÇ≥Ë≤™Ê±°Ë°åÁÇ∫ÔºåËªçÊîø‰∫∫Âì°Á¥ÄÂæãÊïóÂ£û„ÄÅÂØ¶ÊñΩÁµ±Âà∂Á∂ìÊøüÈôêÂà∂Ê∞ëÈñìÁ∂ìÊøüÊ¥ªÂãïÔºõÂúãÊ∞ëÊîøÂ∫úÂ∞áÂú®Ëá∫ÁÅ£ÊâÄÊé•Êî∂ÁöÑÁâ©Ë≥áÁÑ°ÂÑüÈÅãÂæÄ‰∏≠ÂúãÂ§ßÈô∏ÔºåÊäïÂÖ•ÂúãÂÖ±Êà∞Áà≠ÔºåÁâ©ÂÉπ‰∏äÊº≤ÂçÅÂÄç‰ª•‰∏äÔºåÂèäÊø´Âç∞Ëá∫Âπ£Êáâ‰ªòË≤°ÊîøÊîØÂá∫ÔºåÂ∞éËá¥ÈÄöË≤®ËÜ®ËÑπÂö¥ÈáçÔºåÂΩ±ÈüøËá∫ÁÅ£Á∂ìÊøüÁîöÊ∑±ÔºåÂºïÁôºÊ∞ëÊÄ®„ÄÇÔºàÂê≥ËÅ∞ÊïèÔºå2006Ôºå129-159Ôºâ

          Âü∫ÊñºÁ¥¢Ë≥†ÂéüÂâáÔºåÊó•Êú¨Áïô‰∏ãÁöÑ237ÂÆ∂ÂúãÊúâ‰ºÅÊ•≠ËàáÁßÅ‰∫∫ÂÖ¨Âè∏„ÄÅ600È§òÂÄãÂñÆ‰ΩçÔºåÁ¥çÂÖ•Èï∑ÂÆòÂÖ¨ÁΩ≤ÂêÑÂ±ÄËôïË®≠ÁΩÆÁöÑ27ÂÆ∂ÂÖ¨Âè∏Á∂ìÁáüÔºå‰ΩÜÂú®Â§ßË¶èÊ®°Êé•Êî∂‰∏çÁï∂Ëàá‰∫∫Ë¨Ä‰∏çËáß‰∏ãÔºåÊó•Êú¨Ë≤°Áî¢ËÆäÊàêÁâπÊ¨äËÄÖÁöÑÁßÅÊúâË≤°„ÄÅÁîöËá≥ÈÅ≠Ê®ôÂîÆÔºåËá∫ÁÅ£‰∫∫ÁöÑÁßÅÊúâË≤°ËàáÁî¢Ê•≠‰πüË¢´ÁÑ°ÁêÜÊ≤íÊî∂„ÄÇÔºàË≥¥Êæ§Ê∂µÔºå1994Ôºå3-27Ôºâ

          ÂúãÊ∞ëÊîøÂ∫úË¢´Êâπ‰ª•„ÄåÂæÅÊúçËÄÖ„Äç‰πãÂßøÂÖ•‰∏ªÂè∞ÁÅ£„ÄÅÊá∑Êä±Ëëó„ÄåÂÑ™Ë∂äÊÑü„ÄçÂ∞àÊ©´Êø´Ê¨äÔºåËá∫ÁÅ£‰∫∫È£ΩÂèó‰æÜÂè∞Â§ßÈô∏ËªçÊîø‰∫∫Âì°Ê≠ßË¶ñÔºå‰∏¶Ëôï‰ª•Á®ÆÁ®Æ‰∏çÂÖ¨Âπ≥ÁöÑÂ∞çÂæÖÔºåÊôÆÈÅçÂºïÁôºËá∫ÁÅ£‰∫∫Â∞çÊñºÂúãÊ∞ëÊîøÂ∫úÁöÑ‰∏çÊªøÔºåÂ∞éËá¥Âæå‰æÜÊ∞ëÊÄ®Á∏ΩÁàÜÁôº„ÄÇÔºàÈÑßÂ≠îÊò≠Ôºå 1991Ôºå29Ôºâ1947Âπ¥2Êúà27Êó•Ëá≥5Êúà16Êó•ÈñìÔºåËá∫ÁÅ£ÂêÑÂú∞ÁàÜÁôºÊøÄÁÉàÁöÑÂÆòÊ∞ëË°ùÁ™ÅÔºåÊ∞ëÁúæË¶ÅÊ±ÇÊîøÊ≤ªÊîπÈù©ÔºåÊúÄÁµÇ‰∏≠ËèØÊ∞ëÂúãÂúãÊ∞ëÊîøÂ∫úÊ¥æÈÅ£ËªçÈöäÊ≠¶ÂäõÈéÆÂ£ìÔºåÂè≤Á®±‰∫å‰∫åÂÖ´‰∫ã‰ª∂ÔºåË®±Â§öÊúüÂæÖÂúãÂ∫úÁµ±Ê≤ªÂèäÂèçÊäóÊó•Êú¨ÁöÑÁü•Ë≠ò‰ªΩÂ≠êÁΩπÈõ£ÊàñË¢´Ëø´ÂÆ≥ËÄåÂÆ¢Ê≠ªÁï∞ÈÑâ„ÄÇ

          The Nationalist Government‚Äôs Military Takeover of Taiwan

          On August 15, 1945, Japan announced its acceptance of the Potsdam Declaration and surrendered. Following the war, the Nationalist government, under the General Order No. One issued by the Allied forces, was granted the authority to accept Japan‚Äôs surrender in Taiwan, which had not previously been part of China‚Äôs military zone. On October 25, 1945, the Republic of China‚Äôs Nationalist government officially took over Taiwan and the Penghu Islands, marking the end of the Japanese colonial period and the beginning of the postwar era.

          However, shortly after Taiwan‚Äôs so-called recovery (guangfu), the government became plagued by widespread corruption, military and political personnel exhibited poor discipline, and the administration imposed a controlled economy that restricted private economic activity. The Nationalist government transferred resources seized in Taiwan to mainland China to support its ongoing civil war against the Chinese Communist Party. This resulted in a more than tenfold increase in prices, exacerbated by the excessive printing of Taiwanese currency to cover government expenses, leading to severe inflation and profoundly impacting Taiwan‚Äôs economy, fueling public resentment.

          (Wu Tsung-Min, 2006, pp. 129-159)

          Following the principle of postwar reparations, 237 state-owned and private Japanese enterprises, along with over 600 industrial units, were placed under the management of 27 companies established within various bureaus of the Taiwan Provincial Administrative Office. However, due to large-scale mismanagement and corruption, these Japanese assets were transformed into private property for the privileged elite or were auctioned off. Taiwanese private property and businesses were also unjustly confiscated.

          (Lai Tze-Han, 1994, pp. 3-27)

          The Nationalist government was widely criticized for ruling Taiwan with the attitude of a conqueror, governing with a sense of superiority and arbitrary authority. The Taiwanese population suffered from discrimination and unfair treatment by the newly arrived mainland Chinese military and political officials, leading to widespread dissatisfaction with the Nationalist government. This mounting resentment eventually erupted into large-scale protests.

          (Teng Kung-Chao, 1991, p. 29)

          Between February 27 and May 16, 1947, violent clashes between government officials and civilians broke out across Taiwan as citizens demanded political reforms. In response, the Nationalist government deployed military forces to suppress the unrest, in what became known as the February 28 Incident (Er-er-ba Shijian). Many intellectuals who had initially welcomed Nationalist rule and resisted Japanese colonialism were either killed, persecuted, or forced into exile, dying away from their homeland.

Âè∞ÁÅ£Âü∫Áù£Èï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉÂçó„ÄÅÂåóÂ§ßÊúÉÁÇ∫Ë¶Å‰øùÈöúÂÖ∂ÊïôÊúÉÂú∞‰ΩçÔºå1942Âπ¥ÂòóË©¶‰ª•„ÄåÂè∞ÁÅ£Â§ßÊúÉ„ÄçÂêçÁæ©ÂâçÂæÄÊó•Êú¨‰∫§Ê∂âÂä†ÂÖ•Êó•Êú¨Âü∫Áù£ÊïôÂúòÊú™Êûú„ÄÇ1943Âπ¥2Êúà26Êó•ÔºåÂçóÂåóÊïôÊúÉÂÖàË°åËÅØÂêàÔºåÊé°ÊïôÂçÄÂà∂ÂàÜÁÇ∫Âè∞Âåó„ÄÅ‰∏≠ÈÉ®„ÄÅÂè∞Âçó„ÄÅÈ´òÈõÑ4ÊïôÂçÄÔºõÁÑ∂ÂæåÊñº1944Âπ¥4Êúà29Êó•ÔºåÁî±Êó•Êú¨Âü∫Áù£ÊïôÂúòÂè∞ÁÅ£ÊïôÂçÄ„ÄÅÊó•Êú¨ËÅñÂÖ¨ÊúÉÂè∞ÁÅ£ÂÇ≥ÈÅìÂçÄÂèäÂè∞ÁÅ£Âü∫Áù£Èï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉÁ∏ΩÊúÉ‰∏âÂñÆ‰ΩçÂêà‰ΩµËÄåÊàê„ÄåÊó•Êú¨Âü∫Áù£ÊïôÂè∞ÁÅ£ÊïôÂúò„ÄçÔºåÂè∞ÁÅ£Âü∫Áù£ÊïôÂÆåÂÖ®Ê≠∏ÊñºÂêå‰∏ÄÊåáÊèÆÁ≥ªÁµ±ÔºåÂéüÂ±¨Èï∑ËÄÅÊúÉÁ≥ªÁµ±ÁöÑÂçóÂåóÊïôÊúÉÈáçÂøÉÁßªËΩâËá≥Âè∞Âåó„ÄÇ1944Âπ¥7ÊúàÂæåÔºåË®±Â§öÁâßÂ∏´‰πüË¢´ÂæÅÂè¨ÁÇ∫„ÄåË≠∑ÂúãÊå∫Ë∫´ÈöäÂì°„Äç1944Âπ¥11ÊúàÔºåÂè∞ÂåóËøëÈÉäÂÇ≥ÈÅìÂ∏´ÁµÑÊàê„ÄåÊà∞ÊôÇÊÖ∞ÂïèÂêàÂî±Âúò„ÄçÔºå 1945Âπ¥2Êúà13Êó•ÔºåÂè∞ÁÅ£ÊïôÂúòÁµÑ„ÄåÊà∞ÊôÇÊå∫Ë∫´Èöä„ÄçÔºåË¶ÅÊ±ÇÈöäÂì°Êèê‰æõÊÉÖÂ†±Ëá≥Á∏ΩÈÉ®Ôºå1945Âπ¥8Êúà15Êó•Êà∞Áà≠ÁµêÊùüÔºå„ÄåÊó•Êú¨Âü∫Áù£ÊïôÂè∞ÁÅ£ÊïôÂúò„ÄçËß£Êï£ÔºåÈÅéÂéªÂÇ≥ÊïôËÄÖÂøÖÈ†àÁÇ∫ÊîøÂ∫úÊïàÂäõÁöÑÈõôÈáçË∫´ÂàÜ‰πüÊâçÂæó‰ª•Ëß£Èô§„ÄÇÔºàÂæêË¨ô‰ø°Ôºå1965Ôºå265-266Ôºâ

Wartime Policies on Religion in Taiwan (1940-1950)

During wartime, the Japanese government not only granted State Shinto a privileged status but also tightened control over other religions. The most comprehensive regulation was the Religious Juridical Persons Act (ÂÆóÊïôÊ≥ï‰∫∫Ê≥ï, Êò≠Âíå26Âπ¥Ê≥ïÂæãÁ¨¨126Âè∑), enacted in 1939, which encompassed all ‚ÄúShinto sects, Buddhist sects, religious organizations, churches, religious groups, dioceses, and other similar organizations‚Äù (Article 2-1). After the Public Security Preservation Law (Ê≤ªÂÆâÁ∂≠ÊåÅÊ≥ï) was revised in 1941, penalties were intensified, and the scope of suppression was expanded, further increasing religious persecution.

By that time, Japan had entered a full-scale wartime mobilization system, especially following the enactment of the National Mobilization Law (ÂõΩÂÆ∂Á∑èÂãïÂì°Ê≥ï) in 1938, which integrated Japan‚Äôs national economy into wartime conditions. The legal framework also promoted National Spiritual Mobilization (ÂõΩÊ∞ëÁ≤æÁ•ûÁ∑èÂãïÂì°), making it difficult for religious activities and doctrines to remain unaffected. The religious control policies were guided by the following principles:

  1. Upholding the national polity and eliminating irreverent ideologies.

    1. Preventing public unrest caused by religious beliefs.

      1. Maintaining fundamental economic structures.

        1. Prohibiting morally disruptive behaviors.

          (Sasaki Toshiji, 1966, p.49; Nagai Kiyoko, 1965, p.237)

          Suppression of Religious Practices in Taiwan

          In 1936, Governor-General of Taiwan, Kobayashi Seiz≈ç, held the Grand Shrine Amulet Distribution Ceremony at the official residence, emphasizing the importance of household worship and faith. Following this, local authorities initiated campaigns to suppress traditional Taiwanese beliefs, including the Official Hall Reform Movement and the Temple Reorganization Movement. The Official Hall Reform Movement primarily required every household to install a kamidana (Shinto household altar) and enshrine the Grand Shrine Amulet (jing≈´ taima). Meanwhile, the handling of ancestral tablets and religious statues varied by locality.

          (Tsai Chin-Tang, 1991, pp. 65-72)

          For Christians, the conflict between their faith and state ideology became increasingly apparent. The Northern Presbyterian Church‚Äôs declaration on the national situation was seen as overly sycophantic. On the issue of Emperor worship, the Northern Church frequently justified participation by claiming that ‚Äúnational rituals are non-religious or transcend religious patriotism.‚Äù They also took the initiative to sever ties with their Canadian mother church.

          Another major challenge was the Shinto shrine worship requirement for Christian schools. Throughout the 1930s, churches in the north and south adopted different approaches to address this challenge. By 1940, due to its religious stance, Tainan Theological Seminary refused to compromise with the authorities, leading to its closure and merger with Taipei Theological Seminary. All Western missionaries were forced to return to their home countries, and Taiwanese churches entered a phase of complete self-governance, self-propagation, and self-financing.

          In November 1940, the Japan Christian Alliance held its 18th General Assembly, officially admitting the Taiwanese church. However, under the influence of State Shinto, the government organized three Christian Missionary Training Conferences across Taiwan starting that year. Presbyterian pastors and preachers were compelled to attend these sessions, where they were indoctrinated with Shinto principles and nationalistic ideologies. Those forced to participate, such as Pastors Huang Wu-Tung and Hsu Chien-Hsin, later documented their suffering in written accounts.

          (Hsu Chien-Hsin, 1965, pp. 262-264)

          The Pacific War and Christian Repression

          In 1941, with the outbreak of the Pacific War, the Government-General of Taiwan promoted the concepts of Loyalty and Sacrifice and Selfless Devotion, establishing the Imperial Subjects Service Association (ÁöáÊ∞ëÂ•âÂÖ¨‰ºö), which integrated the state, Shinto, the Emperor, and the Japanese spirit, further suppressing traditional religious practices while expanding societal mobilization in Taiwan.

          In 1942, this organization expanded into the Taiwan Christian Service Association, later renamed the Taiwan Christian Service Corps, with the ultimate goal of merging all Christian groups into a single Taiwan Christian Church.

          (Hsu Chien-Hsin, 1965, pp. 262-264)

          In an effort to secure their position, the Northern and Southern Synods of the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan attempted in 1942 to join the United Church of Japan under the name Taiwan Assembly, but their request was rejected. On February 26, 1943, the northern and southern churches united under a diocesan system, dividing Taiwan into four districts: Taipei, Central, Tainan, and Kaohsiung.

          Then, on April 29, 1944, the United Church of Japan Taiwan Diocese, the Taiwan District of the Nippon Sei Ko Kai (Anglican Church), and the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan were forcibly merged to form the Japan Christian Church Taiwan Diocese, bringing all Taiwanese Christian institutions under a single command structure. The leadership of the Presbyterian Church shifted to Taipei.

          After July 1944, many pastors were conscripted into the National Defense Mobilization Corps. In November 1944, preachers near Taipei formed a Wartime Consolation Choir. On February 13, 1945, the Taiwan Christian Service Corps established a Wartime Mobilization Unit, requiring members to provide intelligence reports to headquarters.

          With Japan‚Äôs surrender on August 15, 1945, the Japan Christian Church Taiwan Diocese was dissolved, and the obligation of Christian missionaries to serve the government was finally lifted.

          (Hsu Chien-Hsin, 1965, pp. 265-266)

          1. Ë´ãÊèèËø∞ÈÄôÂçÅÂπ¥Èñì‰∏≠ÂúãÁ§æÊúÉÈóúÊñºÊïµË¶ñÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÁöÑÁöÑÁõ∏ÈóúÊÉÖÊ≥ÅÔºåÂåÖÊã¨ËàáÁâπÂÆöÂúòÈ´îÁöÑË°ùÁ™ÅÂíåËûçÂêàÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÁöÑÂä™ÂäõÊÉÖÊ≥ÅÁ≠â ÔºàÊØèÂÄã‰∫ã‰ª∂500-1,000Â≠óÔºâ„ÄÇ ‚òí

            1. Ë´ãÂú®ÊñáÊ™îÊú´„ÄåÊñáÁçª„Äç ËôïÊ∑ªÂä†Áõ∏ÈóúÊñáÁçªÁöÑÂá∫ËôïÔºàËá≥Â∞ë‰∏ÄËôïÂá∫ËôïÔºåË∂äÂ§öË∂äÂ•Ω„ÄÇ Â¶ÇÂèØËÉΩÔºå‰πüË´ãÂåÖÊã¨‰∏≠Êó•ÈüìËã±ÊàñÂÖ∂‰ªñÊ≠êÊ¥≤Ë™ûË®ÄÁöÑÊñáÁçªÂá∫ËôïÔºâ„ÄÇ ‚òí

          ÊÇ®ÁöÑÂõûÁ≠îÔºö

          Êú¨ÊôÇÊúüÁÇ∫Êó•Êú¨ÊôÇ‰ª£Âà∞ÂúãÊ∞ëÊîøÂ∫ú‰æÜÂè∞Èñì‰πãÈÅéÊ∏°ÊôÇÊúüÔºåÊó•Êú¨Âú®‰∫åÊà∞ÊúÄÂæåÁöÑÁöáÊ∞ëÂåñÊîøÁ≠ñÊñºÂâç‰∏ÄÊôÇÊúüÂ∑≤Ë®éË´ñÔºåËÄåÂúãÊ∞ëÈª®ÊîøÂ∫úÂú®Âè∞Â∞çÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÁöÑÂÑ™ÂæÖ‰πüÂ∞öÊú™ÊòéÈ°ØÂá∫ÁèæÔºåÊîøÊ≤ªÁ¥õÊìæÁöÑÂ§ßÁí∞Â¢É‰∏ãÂú®Âè∞ÁÅ£ÁÑ°ÊòéÈ°ØÂ∞çÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÁöÑÁ§æÊúÉÊïµË¶ñÊÉÖÁ∑íÂíåË°åÂãï„ÄÇ

          This period marks the transition from Japanese rule to the arrival of the Nationalist government in Taiwan. The final K≈çminka (imperialization) policies implemented by Japan during World War II were discussed in the previous section, while the preferential treatment of Christianity by the Kuomintang (KMT) government in Taiwan had yet to become evident. Amidst the political turbulence, there were no significant manifestations of social hostility or actions against Christianity in Taiwan during this transitional phase.

          3.Âú∞Á∑£ÊîøÊ≤ªÁí∞Â¢ÉÂíåÂ§ñ‰∫§Èóú‰øÇ

          Ë´ãÂú®‰∏ãÊñπ „ÄåÊÇ®ÁöÑÂõûÁ≠î„Äç ÈÉ®ÂàÜÊèèËø∞Âú®ÈÄôÂçÅÂπ¥ÈñìÔºåÊúÄÊúâÂΩ±ÈüøÂäõÁöÑÂú∞Á∑£ÊîøÊ≤ªÂãïÊÖãÂíåÂ§ñ‰∫§Èóú‰øÇ„ÄÇ

          ÔºàË´ãÂú®ÂÆåÊàêÁõ∏ÊáâÁöÑË¶ÅÊ±ÇÂêéÊ®ôË®òÊñáÂêéË§áÈÅ∏Ê°ÜÔºâÔºö

          The Nationalist Government‚Äôs Military Takeover of Taiwan

          On August 15, 1945, Japan announced its acceptance of the Potsdam Declaration and surrendered. Following the war, the Nationalist government, under the General Order No. One issued by the Allied forces, was granted the authority to accept Japan‚Äôs surrender in Taiwan, which had not previously been part of China‚Äôs military zone. On October 25, 1945, the Republic of China‚Äôs Nationalist government officially took over Taiwan and the Penghu Islands, marking the end of the Japanese colonial period and the beginning of the postwar era.

          However, shortly after Taiwan‚Äôs so-called recovery (guangfu), the government became plagued by widespread corruption, military and political personnel exhibited poor discipline, and the administration imposed a controlled economy that restricted private economic activity. The Nationalist government transferred resources seized in Taiwan to mainland China to support its ongoing civil war against the Chinese Communist Party. This resulted in a more than tenfold increase in prices, exacerbated by the excessive printing of Taiwanese currency to cover government expenses, leading to severe inflation and profoundly impacting Taiwan‚Äôs economy, fueling public resentment.

          (Wu Tsung-Min, 2006, pp. 129-159)

          Following the principle of postwar reparations, 237 state-owned and private Japanese enterprises, along with over 600 industrial units, were placed under the management of 27 companies established within various bureaus of the Taiwan Provincial Administrative Office. However, due to large-scale mismanagement and corruption, these Japanese assets were transformed into private property for the privileged elite or were auctioned off. Taiwanese private property and businesses were also unjustly confiscated.

          (Lai Tze-Han, 1994, pp. 3-27)

          The Nationalist government was widely criticized for ruling Taiwan with the attitude of a conqueror, governing with a sense of superiority and arbitrary authority. The Taiwanese population suffered from discrimination and unfair treatment by the newly arrived mainland Chinese military and political officials, leading to widespread dissatisfaction with the Nationalist government. This mounting resentment eventually erupted into large-scale protests.

          (Teng Kung-Chao, 1991, p. 29)

          Between February 27 and May 16, 1947, violent clashes between government officials and civilians broke out across Taiwan as citizens demanded political reforms. In response, the Nationalist government deployed military forces to suppress the unrest, in what became known as the February 28 Incident (Er-er-ba Shijian). Many intellectuals who had initially welcomed Nationalist rule and resisted Japanese colonialism were either killed, persecuted, or forced into exile, dying away from their homeland.
